class="mantine-Grid-col mantine-3816o2"><div class="mantine-Container-root mantine-i7qrnu" id="post" data-component="post-content"><div class="RichText_wrapper__xZRh7"><p>Our threat hunters have been busy searching for abuse of the recently-released zero-day remote code execution bug in Microsoft Office (<code>CVE-2022-30190</code>). As part of their investigations, they found evidence of a threat actor hosting malicious payloads on what appears to be an <code>Australian VOIP telecommunications provider</code> with a presence in the <code>South Pacific nation of Palau</code>.</p><p>Further analysis indicated that targets in <code>Palau</code> were sent malicious documents that, when opened, exploited this vulnerability, causing victim computers to contact the provider’s website, download and execute the malware, and subsequently become infected.</p><p><strong>Key Observations</strong></p><p>This threat was a complex multi-stage operation utilizing <code>LOLBAS</code> (Living off the Land Binaries And Scripts), which allowed the attacker to initialize the attack using the <code>CVE-2022-30190</code> vulnerability within the <code>Microsoft Support Diagnostic Tool</code>. This vulnerability enables threat actors to run malicious code without the user downloading an executable to their machine which might be detected by endpoint detection.</p><p>Multiple stages of this malware were signed with a legitimate company certificate to add additional legitimacy and minimize the chance of detection.</p><p><strong>First stage</strong></p><p>The compromised website, as pictured in the screenshot below, was used to host <code>robots.txt</code> which is an executable which was disguised as “robots.txt”. We believe the name was used to conceal itself from detection if found in network logs. Using the Diagnostics Troubleshooting Wizard (<code>msdt.exe</code>), this file “robots.txt” was downloaded and saved as the file (<code>Sihost.exe</code>) and then executed.</p></div><div class="RichText_wrapper__xZRh7"><p><strong>Second Stage, Sihost.exe</strong></p><p>When the renamed “robots.txt” – “Sihost.exe” – was executed by msdt.exe it downloaded the second stage of the attack which was a loader with the hash <code>b63fbf80351b3480c62a6a5158334ec8e91fecd057f6c19e4b4dd3febaa9d447</code>. This executable was then used to download and decrypt the third stage of the attack, an encrypted file stored as ‘<code>favicon.svg</code>’ on the same web server.</p><p><strong>Third stage, favicon.svg</strong></p><p>After this file has been decrypted, it is used to download the fourth stage of the attack from <code>palau.voipstelecom.com[.]au.</code> These files are named <code>Sevntx64.exe</code> and <code>Sevntx.lnk</code>, which are then executed on the victims’ machine.</p></div><div class="mantine-Container-root mantine-1yr9cmk"><picture id="the-picture"><source points\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003eAuraStealer is an emerging malware-as-a-service (MaaS) infostealer observed in recent Scam-Yourself campaigns.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eTo hinder both static and dynamic analysis, AuraStealer employs a wide range of anti-analysis and obfuscation techniques, including indirect control flow obfuscation and exception-driven API hashing.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eIn this technical blog post, we provide a deep dive into the malware’s execution flow and functionality and provide practical tips and workflows for defeating its obfuscation.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eTo support the defense community, Gen Threat Labs provides multiple scripts to assist defenders in analyzing AuraStealer, including a string decryption script and a dedicated configuration extractor.\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003ch2\u003eIntroduction\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eAuraStealer is a rapidly growing infostealer-as-a-service, actively promoted across multiple underground forums since July 2025. The stealer is developed in C++ with a build size of ~500-700 kB and targets Windows systems from Windows 7 to Windows 11. It is marketed as a supposedly highly efficient, low-footprint stealer capable of stealing data from more than 110 browsers, 70 applications (including wallets and 2FA tools), as well as over 250 browser extensions, with the ability to further expand its collection scope through a customizable configuration. Contrary to the advertised claims, AuraStealer still contains multiple flaws that undermine its stealth and evasion capabilities, offering clear detection opportunities for defenders.\u003c/p\u003e"},{"bundle":"image_inline","images":[{"sources":[{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_medium/public/2025-12/aurastealer_figure_01%20copy.jpg.webp?itok=eu_bI5eY 1x","media":"(min-width: 720px)","type":"image/webp","width":1076,"height":592},{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_small/public/2025-12/aurastealer_figure_01%20copy.jpg.webp?itok=z5Zd256e 1x","media":"(min-width: 420px)","type":"image/webp","width":720,"height":396}],"caption":"Figure 1: AuraStealer promoted on an underground forum.","url":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_small/public/2025-12/aurastealer_figure_01%20copy.jpg.webp?itok=z5Zd256e","link":""}]},{"bundle":"rich_text","content":"\u003cp\u003eThe infostealer is offered through a tiered subscription model, with the \u003cstrong\u003eBasic\u003c/strong\u003e \u003cstrong\u003eplan\u003c/strong\u003e priced at $295/month and the \u003cstrong\u003eAdvanced\u003c/strong\u003e \u003cstrong\u003eplan\u003c/strong\u003e at $585/month. The tiers differ primarily in terms of configuration flexibility, data filtering, and operational scalability. A third tier, \u003cstrong\u003eTeam plan\u003c/strong\u003e, is currently under development and is expected to introduce features tailored for collaborative use. Additionally, a temporary \u003cstrong\u003eTrial tier\u003c/strong\u003e was also offered, priced at $165 for a two-week subscription, which included all the features of the \u003cstrong\u003eBasic plan\u003c/strong\u003e. The \u003cstrong\u003eTrial tier\u003c/strong\u003e was available for purchase only during a 30-day window, aiming to attract new users before they committed to a full subscription.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe subscriptions include access to a dedicated web panel for managing and viewing stolen data, built on the Tabler template. Given that both the panel and the stealer were originally offered exclusively in Russian, it is reasonable to assume the developers operate within Russian‑speaking cybercriminal communities. However, this does not necessarily indicate their precise origin. The panel has since been updated and now supports both Russian and English.\u003c/p\u003e"},{"bundle":"image_inline","images":[{"sources":[{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_medium/public/2025-12/aurastealer_figure_02.jpeg.webp?itok=zH6rBsfy primarily spreads through Scam-Yourself campaigns, with\u0026nbsp;\u003ca href=\"https://undercodenews.com/tiktoks-dark-trick-aura-stealer-malware-masquerades-as-product-activation-guides/\" target=\"_blank\"\u003eTikTok videos\u003c/a\u003e disguised as product activation guides being one of them. In these videos, victims are lured by an apparently easy tutorial promising free activation of otherwise paid software. Viewers are instructed to manually retype and run a displayed command in an administrative PowerShell, which, however, instead of activating the software, quietly downloads and executes the malicious payload. Concrete examples of these videos, along with the full execution chain and additional IoCs, can be found\u0026nbsp;\u003ca href=\"https://isc.sans.edu/diary/32380\" target=\"_blank\"\u003ehere\u003c/a\u003e.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eApart from TikTok Scam-Yourself campaigns, AuraStealer is also distributed through supposedly cracked games or software, with delivery chains of varying complexity. In the simplest cases, only a UPX-packed version of AuraStealer is executed, without additional stages, whereas the more elaborate campaigns employ multi-stage execution flows, involving custom loaders, DLL sideloading, injection into legitimate processes, or other intermediary steps. We have also observed AuraStealer being delivered together with GlassWorm via a malicious VS Code extension. Taken together, these observations indicate that AuraStealer is sold purely as the stealer itself, without a loader layer or additional delivery mechanisms.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eDetermining the exact scope of the AuraStealer threat is challenging, as we often block its delivery chains at earlier stages before the final payload can be executed. While the proactive defense is crucial for keeping our users safe, it also results in many AuraStealer payloads never reaching the system. As a result, it becomes inherently difficult not only to observe and quantify the full scope of the threat, but also to determine with certainty whether the blocked payloads were indeed intended to deliver AuraStealer.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eShould it, for example, be delivered through ClickFix, one of the most widely adopted delivery techniques lately, we would proactively defend against those attacks right at the clipboard stage with our\u0026nbsp;\u003ca href=\"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/news/family-of-brands/clipboard-protection\" target=\"_blank\"\u003eClipboard protection\u003c/a\u003e feature. ClickFix typically refers to a technique in which users are presented with artificially generated errors or problems that must be resolved to continue, while simultaneously being provided with step-by-step instructions to address the issue. The steps usually include copying a command and running it via the Windows Run dialog or another system prompt, which, however, rather than resolving the issue, silently retrieves and executes the malicious payload.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eTo summarize, AuraStealer is not yet as widespread and popular as the more established infostealer families currently dominating the threat landscape, such as Lumma Stealer, StealC or Vidar. However, according to an\u0026nbsp;\u003ca href=\"https://g0njxa.medium.com/approaching-stealers-devs-a-brief-interview-with-aura-9b513369e117\" target=\"_blank\"\u003einterview\u003c/a\u003e conducted by\u0026nbsp;\u003ca href=\"https://x.com/g0njxa\" target=\"_blank\"\u003eg0njxa\u003c/a\u003e with the AuraStealer developers, the malware is backed by a committed team that continuously improves and evolves its functionality, aiming to become the number one in the infostealer market. This suggests that AuraStealer could become a more significant player in the future.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eAuraStealer Obfuscation\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eAuraStealer incorporates a wide range of techniques designed to hinder both static and dynamic analysis. These include control flow obfuscation, string encryption, constant obfuscation, as well as standard anti-debug, anti-tamper, and anti-VM checks. It further employs advanced techniques such as exception-driven API-hashing, leveraging the Heaven’s Gate for suspicious NTDLL calls, and performing checks to detect breakpoints on return addresses.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eIndirect Control Flow Obfuscation\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe first obfuscation technique an analyst is likely to encounter when opening AuraStealer in a disassembler, such as IDA Pro, is its indirect control flow obfuscation. The obfuscation is not only immediately noticeable, but also one of the most troublesome obstacles that must be overcome to properly analyze the stealer. The obfuscation is based on systematically replacing direct jumps and calls with indirect ones, where the actual target address is computed only at runtime. This method effectively disrupts static analysis, as the disassemblers are left with a set of seemingly unrelated basic blocks, effectively breaking any control-flow graph analysis or decompilation.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eTo demonstrate how this obfuscation works in practice, and how it can be defeated, we focus on the\u0026nbsp;\u003ccode\u003eWinMain\u003c/code\u003e function, which is both the longest and the most complex function of the stealer. Several examples illustrating the obfuscation mechanism at the assembly level are shown in Figure 4 below, with the instructions relevant to computing each target address highlighted in a distinct color. First, the most basic scheme used to obfuscate function calls, which relies solely on the sum of two values can be resolved with a simple trick in IDA Pro. Moreover, AuraStealer applies the same obfuscation scheme to hide certain function arguments as well, making it applicable for those cases as well.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eTo tackle the more sophisticated patterns, \u003cstrong\u003ebackward slicing\u003c/strong\u003e proved to be the most effective approach. For those interested in a more detailed explanation of how this technique works, the articles \u003ca href=\"https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/lummac2-obfuscation-through-indirect-control-flow\" target=\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cem\u003eLummaC2: Obfuscation Through Indirect Control Flow\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/a\u003e by Nino Isakovic and Chuong Dong, and \u003ca href=\"https://cyber.wtf/2025/11/19/rhadamanthys-loader-deobfuscation/\" target=\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cem\u003eRhadamanthys Loader Deobfuscation\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/a\u003e by Melissa Eckardt provide excellent explanations and are definitely worth reading.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eIn short, backward slicing is a program analysis technique that identifies instructions that may influence a specific register or memory address at a specific program point by following data and control dependencies. The resulting set of instructions (also referred to as “slice”) can then be used for further analysis, such as emulation or symbolic execution, to recover the value of the targeted register or memory location.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eIn our case, we employed a heuristic heavily inspired by backward slicing but simplified and tailored specifically for AuraStealer. The heuristic can be summarized in the following steps:\u003c/p\u003e\u003col\u003e\u003cli\u003eLocate the indirect \u003ccode\u003ejmp/call\u003c/code\u003e instruction. \u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eAdd the register used as the target operand of this indirect \u003ccode\u003ejmp/call\u003c/code\u003e instruction to the \u003ccode\u003etracked_regs\u003c/code\u003e\u003cem\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/em\u003eset.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eIterate through instructions in reverse (from the indirect \u003ccode\u003ejmp/call\u003c/code\u003e towards lower addresses), analyzing each instruction whether it affects any of the tracked registers.\u003col\u003e\u003cli\u003eIf an instruction \u003cstrong\u003emodifies\u003c/strong\u003e a tracked register, mark it as \u003cstrong\u003erelevant\u003c/strong\u003e.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eFor a relevant instruction, determine if it completely overwrites a tracked register (e.g., \u003ccode\u003eMOV reg, imm\u003c/code\u003e, \u003ccode\u003eXOR reg, reg\u003c/code\u003e\u003cem\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e,\u0026nbsp;\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/em\u003eetc.), and if so, remove that register from \u003ccode\u003etracked_reg\u003c/code\u003e, ending its dependency chain.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eOtherwise, identify all source registers of that relevant instruction and add them to the \u003ccode\u003etracked_reg\u003c/code\u003e\u003cem\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/em\u003eset to propagate the dependency further back.\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ol\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eStop the process if any of these conditions occur:\u003col\u003e\u003cli\u003eThe \u003ccode\u003etracked_regs\u003c/code\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/strong\u003eset is empty (all dependencies have been resolved).\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eA control-flow instruction is encountered (\u003ccode\u003ecall\u003c/code\u003e, \u003ccode\u003ejmp\u003c/code\u003e, \u003ccode\u003eret\u003c/code\u003e), indicating a basic block boundary.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eA maximum instruction limit is reached (fail-safe).\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ol\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eFinally, return the address of the earliest (lowest address) relevant instruction found, marking the start of the slice.\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ol\u003e\u003cp\u003eFor clarity, this heuristic is not meant to implement complete backward slicing. It deliberately omits memory‑write tracking and focuses solely on register flows. However, it is important to note that this limitation is intentional, as register-level analysis is sufficient for the deobfuscation of the indirect control flow obfuscation employed by AuraStealer. We use the heuristic solely to identify the slice’s starting address, from which we then perform symbolic execution with Angr to compute the target address(es). That said, if AuraStealer’s obfuscation were to change, the heuristic could be readily extended to also account for memory interactions.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe reason we chose symbolic execution over emulation is that symbolic execution is inherently designed to explore all possible execution paths, making it easier to implement. In terms of computational requirements, it remains feasible since we only symbolically execute small code chunks.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eAttentive readers may have noticed that this approach has a minor imperfection. The heuristic terminates upon encountering a \u003ccode\u003ecall\u003c/code\u003e instruction. However, as shown in Figure 5, the target address of some \u003ccode\u003ejmps\u003c/code\u003e can depend on the results of previous function calls, which may lead to an incorrectly identified slice start. To handle such cases, whenever a register is unconstrained, we assign it a symbolic boolean value (0 or 1), under the assumption that any function influencing such jumps returns a boolean value. Such cases, however, are relatively rare.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe whole process can be automated with a bit of scripting and the recovered target addresses can be pushed back into IDA as comments, which already gives a good baseline for navigating the code.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e"},{"bundle":"image_inline","images":[{"sources":[{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_medium/public/2025-12/aurastealer_figure_11_0.jpeg.webp?itok=xVeq5AHc 1x","media":"(min-width: 720px)","type":"image/webp","width":1076,"height":867},{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_small/public/2025-12/aurastealer_figure_11_0.jpeg.webp?itok=8dhhCtuk 1x","media":"(min-width: 420px)","type":"image/webp","width":720,"height":580}],"caption":"  Figure 11: Recovered target addresses set as comments in IDA Pro.","url":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_small/public/2025-12/aurastealer_figure_11_0.jpeg.webp?itok=8dhhCtuk","link":""}]},{"bundle":"rich_text","content":"\u003cp\u003eHowever, we can go further – our real goal is to make IDA reconstruct the original control flow graph, not just annotate it. Once the target addresses of the indirect \u003ccode\u003ejmps/calls\u003c/code\u003e are retrieved, there are several ways to proceed. One option would be to patch the binary. However, due to the variability of the patterns involved and the presence of an anti‑tamper protection in AuraStealer, we have opted for a different approach.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eInstead of patching the binary, which is destructive and easily detectable by malware, we leveraged the IDA SDK to hook directly into the instruction analysis process. For this purpose, IDA provides \u003ca href=\"https://cpp.docs.hex-rays.com/structpost__event__visitor__t.html\" target=\"_blank\"\u003e\u003ccode\u003epost_event_visitor_t\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/a\u003e, a class that allows listening and reacting to specific events in IDA by overriding the virtual function \u003ccode\u003ehandle_post_event\u003c/code\u003e.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e"},{"bundle":"image_inline","images":[{"sources":[{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_medium/public/2025-12/aurastealer_figure_12.jpeg.webp?itok=86TkldZY 1x","media":"(min-width: 720px)","type":"image/webp","width":1076,"height":107},{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_small/public/2025-12/aurastealer_figure_12.jpeg.webp?itok=TnbqbesL 1x","media":"(min-width: 420px)","type":"image/webp","width":720,"height":72}],"caption":"Figure 12: The declaration of the handle_post_event virtual function.","url":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_small/public/2025-12/aurastealer_figure_12.jpeg.webp?itok=TnbqbesL","link":""}]},{"bundle":"rich_text","content":"\u003cp\u003eAmong all the possible event codes, the interesting one is the \u003ccode\u003eprocessor_t::ev_ana_insn\u003c/code\u003e\u0026nbsp;(analyze instruction), which is triggered right after IDA calls the processor module’s instruction decoder, but before the resulting \u003ccode\u003einsn_t\u003c/code\u003e structure is finalized and committed to the database. At that point, we can inspect, or even modify, the decoded instruction. In other words, we can alter IDA’s perception of the code without ever touching the underlying binary.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eAt this point, several observations are worth highlighting:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003eThe indirect calls almost always have a single target.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eMany conditional jumps have only two targets, one of which is the immediately following instruction.\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cp\u003eResolving the indirect call obfuscation is therefore straightforward. Whenever we intercept the \u003ccode\u003eprocessor_t::ev_ana_insn\u003c/code\u003e event, we check whether the analyzed instruction is an indirect call (\u003ccode\u003ecall reg\u003c/code\u003e). If the target address is known and it is the only possible target, we replace the \u003ccode\u003ecall reg\u003c/code\u003e instruction with \u003ccode\u003ecall \u0026lt;calculated_target_address\u0026gt;\u003c/code\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eFor indirect conditional jumps, it would, in principle, be possible to determine the exact branch type by analyzing the conditional move used to compute the target address and replacing the \u003ccode\u003ejmp\u003c/code\u003e instruction with the corresponding conditional jump instruction. However, we opted for a much simpler and more pragmatic strategy. Many of these indirect jumps are, in fact, only obfuscated loops or \u003ccode\u003eif-then/if-then-else\u003c/code\u003e constructs with one of the target address pointing to the immediately following instruction. So, whenever we encounter an indirect \u003ccode\u003ejmp\u003c/code\u003e instruction with precisely two possible targets, one of which is the fall-through address, we replace the \u003ccode\u003ejmp\u003c/code\u003e instruction with \u003ccode\u003ejnz\u003c/code\u003e (jump if not zero) pointing to the non-fall-through target address.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThis transformation does not yield a perfectly accurate decompilation. However, if we accept the fact that some comparisons may appear as\u0026nbsp;\u003ccode\u003econdition != 0\u003c/code\u003e rather than their exact form, we end up with a control flow that is already much easier to follow. Moreover, whenever the precise condition semantics matter, the original condition can always be examined directly in the disassembly. Although it would be possible to reconstruct and substitute the exact conditional jump for every case, we do not believe it is worth the effort.\u003c/p\u003e"},{"bundle":"image_inline","images":[{"sources":[{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_medium/public/2025-12/aurastealer_figure_13.jpeg.webp?itok=DA2cm_PF It is thus often necessary to manually adjust where the function begins and ends to ensure IDA analyses the whole function.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eException-Driven API Hashing\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eTo obfuscate its usage of WinAPI functions, AuraStealer employs the API hashing technique. For these purposes, it first resolves all the functions required by the binary via PEB-walking and builds two hash-based lookup tables that maps hashes to XOR-masked addresses.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe reason for maintaining two tables is that AuraStealer does not invoke these function directly. Instead, it deliberately triggers an exception, which, however, is intercepted by a custom exception handler. The handler inspects the address from which the exception originates and uses that information to dispatch the appropriate function address.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eTo resolve a specific function, AuraStealer takes the precomputed \u003ccode\u003eMurmurHash3\u003c/code\u003e value (seeded with \u003ccode\u003e0xDEADBEEF\u003c/code\u003e) of the function’s name, re-hashes it with \u003ccode\u003eFNV-1a\u003c/code\u003e, and uses both values to query the first precomputed lookup table. The query returns the XOR-masked address from which the exception is to be thrown, together with the mask used to protect it. AuraStealer then unmasks the address and makes a call to it, intentionally throwing an \u003ccode\u003eEXCEPTION_ACCESS_VIOLATION\u003c/code\u003e, which is intercepted by the custom exception handler.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe exception handler rehashes the originating address using \u003ccode\u003eFNV-1a\u003c/code\u003e and uses both values to query the second precomputed lookup table. This query returns the XOR-masked address of the actual target function, again along with the mask. The two values are XORed together and the resulting address is loaded into the \u003ccode\u003eEIP\u003c/code\u003e register, effectively invoking the requested WinAPI function.\u003c/p\u003e"},{"bundle":"image_inline","images":[{"sources":[{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_medium/public/2025-12/aurastealer_figure_15.jpeg.webp?itok=kowkaGle 1x","media":"(min-width: 720px)","type":"image/webp","width":1076,"height":1008},{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_small/public/2025-12/aurastealer_figure_15.jpeg.webp?itok=Gw3InX6R 1x","media":"(min-width: 420px)","type":"image/webp","width":720,"height":675}],"caption":"Figure 15: Visualized exception-driven API hashing.","url":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_small/public/2025-12/aurastealer_figure_15.jpeg.webp?itok=Gw3InX6R","link":""}]},{"bundle":"rich_text","content":"\u003cp\u003eThe MurmurHash3 values of the target functions are further obfuscated using an XOR mask (see Figure 16). In this example, \u003ccode\u003e0x130B0F3C ^ 0x25A36E25 = 0x36A86119\u003c/code\u003e, which is equal to the value returned by \u003ccode\u003eMurmurHash3(“GetModuleHandleA”, seed: 0xDEADBEEF\u003c/code\u003e)\u003cem\u003e.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/em\u003eSince the result value is produced only by a XOR of two constants, IDA’s decompiler can optimize the expression and directly display the deobfuscated value (see Figure 17).\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThis allows us to precompute the hashes for all API-hashed functions and replace the decompiler-computed constants with the corresponding function names. However, it is important to note that this approach relies on IDA’s decompiler, which may produce incomplete results if the control flow is not fully deobfuscated. That said, the constants could also be recovered by combining backward slicing to isolate the instructions that influence the final constant value, and emulating them with an emulation framework (for example, Unicorn). In this case, removing the obfuscation would be considerably easier than for the indirect control flow obfuscation, as the constant obfuscation is merely an XOR, requiring emulation of only a few instructions. Therefore, the necessary context is distributed across the entire function. Depending on that outcome, it either performs or skips additional checks, which include an anti-tampering check and a dialog box check.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eAnti-Tampering Check\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe anti-tampering check is done using the \u003ccode\u003eMapFileAndCheckSumW\u003c/code\u003e WinAPI function, which computes the file’s checksum and compares it against the value stored in the PE header. If those values differ, AuraStealer stops its execution.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe purpose of this check is to prevent analysts from patching or otherwise altering the executable. With that in mind, analysts should be cautious about any changes made to the binary (including software breakpoints) and remain vigilant during dynamic analysis, as the malware may deliberately exhibit misleading behavior if tampering is detected.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eDialog Box Check\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eSimilar to what \u003ca href=\"https://outpost24.com/blog/lummac2-anti-sandbox-technique-trigonometry-human-detection/\" target=\"_blank\"\u003eLumma\u003c/a\u003e, and more recently also \u003ca href=\"https://research.checkpoint.com/2025/rhadamanthys-0-9-x-walk-through-the-updates/\" target=\"_blank\"\u003eRhadamanthys\u003c/a\u003e, does, if AuraStealer detects that it is being run unprotected, it displays a dialog box prompting the user to enter a code to continue. Unlike Lumma and Rhadamanthys, it requires the user not only to click the OK button but also to correctly enter the displayed code. On the other hand, it does not display any warning message that malware is to be run, which is what both Lumma and Rhadamanthys display in the message box.\u003c/p\u003e"},{"bundle":"image_inline","images":[{"sources":[{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_medium/public/2025-12/aurastealer_figure_27.jpeg.webp?itok=S_XSIdFH 1x","media":"(min-width: 720px)","type":"image/webp","width":262,"height":149},{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_small/public/2025-12/aurastealer_figure_27.jpeg.webp?itok=LE2ksud8 1x","media":"(min-width: 420px)","type":"image/webp","width":262,"height":149}],"caption":"Figure 27: The AuraStealer’s dialog box displayed when running unprotected.","url":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_small/public/2025-12/aurastealer_figure_27.jpeg.webp?itok=LE2ksud8","link":""}]},{"bundle":"rich_text","content":"\u003cp\u003eThis mechanism halts AuraStealer’s execution until the correct value is provided and is aimed at forcing the malware distributors to deliver the stealer with an additional protective layer. However, we were unable to confirm that claim.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eAnti-Sandbox Checks\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eIf all previous checks pass, the process continues to the anti-sandbox stage, which comprises three primary checks.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eFirst, AuraStealer evaluates whether the \u003ccode\u003eSleep\u003c/code\u003e\u003cem\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/em\u003efunction has been hooked or modified. The script is publicly accessible on our GitHub.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eHuman Checks\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe human check comprises two subchecks, both implemented as infinite loops.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe first check monitors whether the user input changes over time. It begins by measuring the number of ticks between two \u003ccode\u003eGetTickCount\u003c/code\u003e calls with a \u003ccode\u003eSleep(1000 ms)\u003c/code\u003e in between. A while loop then repeatedly performs a \u003ccode\u003eSleep(500 ms)\u003c/code\u003e followed by a call to \u003ccode\u003eGetLastInputInfo\u003c/code\u003e, which returns a \u003ccode\u003eLASTINPUTINFO\u003c/code\u003e structure. This structure contains a variable, \u003ccode\u003edwTime\u003c/code\u003e, representing the tick count at which the last input event occurred. Then, another call to \u003ccode\u003eGetTickCount\u003c/code\u003e is made, and \u003ccode\u003edwTime\u003c/code\u003e is subtracted from it. If the result is less than the previously measured number of ticks measured by the one-second \u003ccode\u003eSleep\u003c/code\u003e, the check passes. Otherwise, the loop repeats until the check passes.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe second check begins by a call to \u003ccode\u003eGetForegroundWindow\u003c/code\u003e, which returns a handle to the current foreground window. A while loop then repeatedly calls \u003ccode\u003eSleep(500 ms)\u003c/code\u003e and checks \u003ccode\u003eGetForegroundWindow\u003c/code\u003e again. If the returned handle differs from the initial one, the check passes. Otherwise, the loop continues until the foreground window changes.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eAnti-VM Checks\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe Anti‑VM check consists of multiple subchecks. First, the presence of a hypervisor is checked using the \u003ccode\u003ecpuid\u003c/code\u003e instruction. When \u003ccode\u003ecpuid\u003c/code\u003e is executed with \u003ccode\u003eEAX = 1\u003c/code\u003e, the 31st bit of \u003ccode\u003eECX\u003c/code\u003e indicates the environment – it is \u003ccode\u003e0\u003c/code\u003e on a physical machine and \u003ccode\u003e1\u003c/code\u003e on a virtual machine.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eNext, a combination of the WinAPI functions \u003ccode\u003eGetDesktopWindow\u003c/code\u003e and \u003ccode\u003eGetWindowRect\u003c/code\u003e is used to obtain the \u003ccode\u003eright\u003c/code\u003e variable from the resulting \u003ccode\u003eRECT\u003c/code\u003e structure, which is then compared against \u003ccode\u003e1024\u003c/code\u003e (it is expected to be greater). Finally, \u003ccode\u003eGlobalMemoryStatusEx\u003c/code\u003e is called, and the \u003ccode\u003eullTotalPhys\u003c/code\u003e variable of the returned \u003ccode\u003eMEMORYSTATUSEX\u003c/code\u003e structure is checked to ensure it is not \u003ccode\u003e0\u003c/code\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eIf all of these checks pass, AuraStealer calls \u003ccode\u003eGetSystemInfo\u003c/code\u003e and inspects the returned \u003ccode\u003eSYSTEM_INFO\u003c/code\u003e structure to verify that \u003ccode\u003edwNumberOfProcessors\u003c/code\u003e is not less than four. Generated Mutex\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eTo prevent multiple instances from running simultaneously, AuraStealer utilizes dynamically generated mutexes based on build-specific and time-dependent values. The malware first loads the \u003ccode\u003ebuild_id\u003c/code\u003e value from its configuration (for example, \u003ccode\u003ea0858933-16a7-433f-a9cc-68490ace0576\u003c/code\u003e) and computes a \u003ccode\u003edjb2\u003c/code\u003e hash over it. The hash is then added to the result of an integer division \u003ccode\u003e_Xtime_get_ticks()/36000000000\u003c/code\u003e, which effectively produces a value that changes once per hour. The final sum serves as a seed for a Mersenne-Twister pseudorandom number generator, which is used to generate a random alphanumeric string between 16 and 32 characters in length. The generated string is prepended with \u003ccode\u003eGlobal\\\u003c/code\u003e, forming the final mutex name.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e"},{"bundle":"image_inline","images":[{"sources":[{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_medium/public/2025-12/aurastealer_figure_45.jpeg.webp?itok=oFS-2g35 1x","media":"(min-width: 720px)","type":"image/webp","width":810,"height":389},{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_small/public/2025-12/aurastealer_figure_45.jpeg.webp?itok=TI9zq9bE 1x","media":"(min-width: 420px)","type":"image/webp","width":720,"height":346}],"caption":"Figure 45: AuraStealer's mutex creation (decompiled code).","url":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_small/public/2025-12/aurastealer_figure_45.jpeg.webp?itok=TI9zq9bE","link":""}]},{"bundle":"rich_text","content":"\u003cp\u003eThe fact that the mutex changes every hour highlights that the stealer is designed to run only for a short period of time – a trend that has become increasingly common among modern infostealers. The goal is to execute briefly, steal as much data as possible, and, preferably, remove all traces of its presence.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eApplication-Bound Encryption Bypass\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eTo extract sensitive data from Chromium-based browsers, AuraStealer must first overcome Application-Bound Encryption (ABE), which it achieves by spawning the target browser in headless mode using \u003ccode\u003eCreateProcessW\u003c/code\u003e, with the process created in a suspended state, followed by a code injection. From within the injected context, the malware invokes \u003ccode\u003eIElevator::Decrypt\u003c/code\u003e to decrypt the \u003ccode\u003eapp_bound_encrypted_key\u003c/code\u003e, which enables the decryption of any ABE-protected data, including passwords and cookies.\u003c/p\u003e"},{"bundle":"image_inline","images":[{"sources":[{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_medium/public/2025-12/aurastealer_figure_46.jpeg.webp?itok=NjlxNOxR 1x","media":"(min-width: 720px)","type":"image/webp","width":762,"height":151},{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_small/public/2025-12/aurastealer_figure_46.jpeg.webp?itok=koba6vpa 1x","media":"(min-width: 420px)","type":"image/webp","width":720,"height":143}],"caption":"Figure 46: AuraStealer’s ABE bypass.","url":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_small/public/2025-12/aurastealer_figure_46.jpeg.webp?itok=koba6vpa","link":""}]},{"bundle":"rich_text","content":"\u003cp\u003eThe injection itself is implemented by creating a shared section via \u003ccode\u003eNtCreateSection\u003c/code\u003e and mapping it into the target browser process using \u003ccode\u003eNtMapViewOfSection\u003c/code\u003e, after which the injected payload is executed using \u003ccode\u003eNtCreateThreadEx\u003c/code\u003e. All of the NTDLL calls involved in the injection are executed through Heaven’s Gate, a technique that allows 64-bit code to be executed from a 32-bit process on a 64-bit OS. This ensures both compatibility with 64-bit browsers and also helps evade detection.\u003c/p\u003e"},{"bundle":"image_inline","images":[{"sources":[{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_medium/public/2025-12/aurastealer_figure_47.jpeg.webp?itok=M2ft3vYC 1x","media":"(min-width: 720px)","type":"image/webp","width":483,"height":185},{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_small/public/2025-12/aurastealer_figure_47.jpeg.webp?itok=qwFnafmQ 1x","media":"(min-width: 420px)","type":"image/webp","width":483,"height":185}],"caption":"Figure 47: AuraStealer’s usage of Heaven's Gate.","url":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_small/public/2025-12/aurastealer_figure_47.jpeg.webp?itok=qwFnafmQ","link":""}]},{"bundle":"rich_text","content":"\u003cp\u003eThe injected payload is a position-independent shellcode, slightly modified at runtime for each target browser process. These two files serve as an inter-process communication mechanism between AuraStealer and the injected shellcode. The first file is used to pass the encrypted \u003ccode\u003eapp_bound_encrypted_key\u003c/code\u003e to the injected shellcode, while the second file is used by the shellcode to write back the decrypted key for retrieval by the stealer.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eAlthough AuraStealer is capable of decrypting the \u003ccode\u003eapp_bound_encrypted\u003c/code\u003e key, required to decrypt appbound-encrypted data, from certain Chromium-based browsers, it still does not work reliably across all of them, suggesting that the product has not yet reached a fully polished state.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eNetwork Traffic\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eAuraStealer’s network traffic can be divided into four stages. The stealer first verifies internet connectivity by checking the reachability of \u003ccode\u003e1.1.1.1:53\u003c/code\u003e (Cloudflare). Upon success, it attempts to establish communication with its C2 infrastructure by querying the \u003ccode\u003e/api/live\u003c/code\u003e endpoint and expecting a response\u003ccode\u003e true\u003c/code\u003e. has discovered a novel WhatsApp account takeover campaign that we refer to as GhostPairing Attack. On the surface it looks very simple. When users open it, they see a page that imitates a Facebook viewer and asks them to “verify” before they can see the content.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eWhat follows is a small sequence of steps that many people complete without thinking. At the end of that sequence, an attacker has full access to the victim’s WhatsApp account without stealing a single password.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThis article explains what we observed, how the attack works in practice, which infrastructure is involved, and what users can do to protect themselves.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eWe use the term GhostPairing Attack because the victim is tricked into completing WhatsApp’s own device pairing flow, adding the attacker’s browser as an invisible extra device on their account. There is no password theft or SIM swap – instead, the user approves the attacker themselves by entering a pairing code that looks like normal verification.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eWhat we observed in the wild\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe campaign was first noticed in Czechia, where compromised accounts started sending short, informal messages to local contacts. The wording varied slightly, but the structure remained the same. The messages were brief, usually mentioning a photo, and included a link that rendered as a Facebook element inside WhatsApp.\u003c/p\u003e"},{"bundle":"image","images":[{"sources":[{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_large/public/2025-12/2.1.png.webp?itok=oGbEG2yY 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_large_2x/public/2025-12/2.1.png.webp?itok=85v0XbWP 2x","media":"(min-width: 1020px)","type":"image/webp","width":386,"height":242},{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_medium/public/2025-12/2.1.png.webp?itok=k3pAs_YA 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_medium_2x/public/2025-12/2.1.png.webp?itok=1tOUP9yY 2x","media":"(min-width: 720px)","type":"image/webp","width":386,"height":242},{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small/public/2025-12/2.1.png.webp?itok=AbtY1QCB 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small_2x/public/2025-12/2.1.png.webp?itok=B_abS2P5 2x","media":"(min-width: 420px)","type":"image/webp","width":386,"height":242}],"caption":"Another lure message sent via WhatsApp","url":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small/public/2025-12/2.1.png.webp?itok=AbtY1QCB","link":""}]},{"bundle":"rich_text","content":"\u003cp\u003eThe domains behind these links were not Facebook at all. Instead, they belonged to a cluster of lookalike sites with names related to photos or posts, for example:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003ephotobox[.]life\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003epostsphoto[.]life\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eyourphoto[.]life\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003ephotopost[.]live\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eyourphoto[.]world\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003etop-foto[.]life\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003efotoface[.]top\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cp\u003ePaths often included strings like /login/post.com or /login/facepost.com, which helped maintain the illusion that the user was dealing with some kind of Facebook login or content viewer.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eAlthough our earliest sightings are in\u003ca\u003e Czech\u003c/a\u003e, nothing in the attack depends on the language. The same template can be reused for any country simply by changing the text of the lure.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eThe fake viewer that is not about Facebook at all\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eWhen a user clicks the link within the WhatsApp message, they land on a very minimal page. It usually carries the Facebook logo and colors, a stripped down layout and a button that invites them to continue or verify before they can see the photo.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThis page has two purposes.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u0026nbsp;First, it creates a sense of familiarity that encourages the user to trust the page. People expect Facebook to ask for some kind of confirmation from time to time. Seeing a login button or a verification step feels normal.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eSecond, it acts as the attacker’s control panel. The page is not connecting with Facebook but rather mediating between the victim and the legitimate WhatsApp Web infrastructure that the attacker is abusing.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eDepending on the variant, the next screen either:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003eshows a QR code and tells the user to scan it with WhatsApp, or\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eshows a numeric code and tells the user to enter it into WhatsApp to confirm a login.\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cp\u003eBoth flows lead to the same outcome, but later we’ll show why the QR code is rarely used in favor of the numeric code. The attacker gets their browser registered as a linked device on the victim’s WhatsApp account, where they have complete control over personal messages, photos and everything else a person does on the WhatsApp platform.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eA quick primer on WhatsApp device linking/pairing\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eTo understand the abuse, it helps to look briefly at how device linking in WhatsApp is \u003cem\u003esupposed\u003c/em\u003e to work.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eWhen people use WhatsApp Web or the desktop client, they are not creating a separate account. They are linking a new device to their existing account. WhatsApp offers more than one way to do this:\u003c/p\u003e\u003col\u003e\u003cli\u003eThe one most people know: open WhatsApp, scan a QR code shown on the desktop or browser, and approve the new session.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eA less visible option: link a device using the account’s phone number and a numeric pairing code that the user has to confirm in the app.\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ol\u003e\u003cp\u003eBoth are legitimate features, and both are meant to make it easier to connect a new laptop or browser without constantly re-entering credentials. Additionally, both can be repurposed if someone convinces the account owner to cooperate.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe campaign we analyzed does exactly that.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eQR based \u003c/strong\u003e\u003ca\u003e\u003cstrong\u003elinking\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e – possible, but a fringe scenario\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eOn paper, attackers could run the same scam using WhatsApp’s QR based device linking. In our tests, this works technically, but it is much less practical for real victims.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eTo abuse this flow, an attacker would open WhatsApp Web in their own browser, capture the QR code shown there and embed it into the fake Facebook viewer page. The victim would then be told to open WhatsApp, go to Linked devices and scan that QR in order to “view the photo”. From WhatsApp’s perspective, the account owner has just approved a new linked device, and the attacker’s browser receives a fully authorized session.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eIn practice, however, there is a big usability problem. Most people run both WhatsApp and the browser on the same phone. Scanning a QR that is displayed on the very device you are using is awkward at best, and often impossible without a second screen, another phone or a tablet. We have all seen edge cases where this happens in legitimate contexts, but it is not something you would build a mass scam around.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThat is why, in the activity we have observed, attackers rely on the phone number and pairing code flow instead. It produces the same end result but feels like a normal two step verification process and can be completed entirely on a single device, which makes it far more attractive for large scale abuse.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eAbusing phone number and pairing codes\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe numeric code variant is slightly more complex and arguably more dangerous, because it resembles two factor authentication even more closely. It is also the most common way the scam plays out.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eHere, the fake page encourages the user to enter their phone number. It then acts as a proxy to the real WhatsApp device linking endpoint.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe behind-the-scenes sequence looks like this:\u003c/p\u003e\u003col\u003e\u003cli\u003eThe victim types their phone number on the fake page.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eThe page forwards that number to WhatsApp’s legitimate “link device via phone number” feature.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eWhatsApp generates a pairing code that is meant to be seen only by the account owner.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eThe attacker’s site takes that code and displays it back to the victim with text that suggests they should “enter this in WhatsApp to confirm the login and see the photo.”\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eThe victim opens WhatsApp, sees the pairing prompt and enters the code, believing they are completing a security check.\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ol\u003e\u003cp\u003eFrom the victim’s point of view, this feels like a standard verification process. They have seen similar flows in many other services.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eFrom WhatsApp’s point of view, the owner of the account has just approved a new linked device using the correct code.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eAgain, the end result is that the attacker’s browser becomes a trusted device for that account.\u003c/p\u003e"},{"bundle":"image","images":[{"sources":[{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_large/public/2025-12/3.png.webp?itok=-qmnBLwA the attacker can do once linked\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eAfter their device is linked, the attacker does not need to exploit anything else. They have the same capabilities that any user has when connecting WhatsApp Web on their own computer.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eIn practice, this includes:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003eReading historical conversations, subject to what has been synced.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eReceiving new messages in real time.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eViewing and downloading media such as photos, videos and voice notes.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eCollecting sensitive information shared between friends: addresses, email addresses, codes, links, which can later be misused or resold.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003esending messages to individuals and groups as if they were the victim.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eforwarding the same lure to many contacts with very little effort.\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cp\u003eThis is not traditional account hijacking in the sense of changing passwords or locking the owner out. The phone continues to work normally. Many victims are unaware that a second device has been added in the background, which is what makes the scam even more dangerous – criminals are hiding in your account, watching your every conversation without you even knowing it.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eIn our observations, that restriction does not consistently clear the linked session. Unless the victim goes into Settings and removes unknown devices, the attacker may retain access.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eHow victims then become GhostPairing attackers\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe propagation mechanism is simple and effective because it is rooted in real relationships.Once an attacker has access to one account, they can use it to distribute the lure text that we mentioned previously to that person’s contacts and groups. Family chats, school groups, sports teams and work-related conversations are all opportunities.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eRecipients see a very short, informal message coming from someone they know. There is no unfamiliar phone number, no obvious formatting errors, no long explanation. This minimal style typically avoids suspicion.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eSome recipients click, some do not. Those who do and complete the linking step become new compromised accounts. They, in turn, can be used to reach fresh sets of contacts that the original attacker did not know existed – the attack expands like a snowball.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThis is what makes the campaign efficient. It does not rely on cold spam. It rides existing trust.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eWhy this approach is concerning\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eSeveral aspects of this technique are worth highlighting.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eFirst, it does not rely on stealing secrets. There is no password phish, no SMS interception, no direct authentication bypass. Everything happens inside the boundaries of the feature set that WhatsApp intended.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eSecond, the lure is highly plausible. For many users, the idea that “Facebook wants you to confirm something in WhatsApp” does not sound obviously wrong. Codes and QR scans have become part of everyday online life, especially on mobile.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThird, it creates persistent access. Linked devices do not disappear when the user closes a tab. They remain active until manually revoked. If there is no habit of checking the list of connected WhatsApp devices, an attacker can remain connected for a long time.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eFinally, it opens the door to more than just simple spam. With access to full conversations and media, an attacker can learn how people talk, who is important to them, and what they might respond to. Voice notes and photos can later be reused in fraud attempts and impersonation, including deepfake enhanced scams, or extortion.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eIn other words, this is a clear example of how social engineering and legitimate infrastructure can combine into a very effective compromise method.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eInfrastructure and kit-like behavior\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eLooking at the domains and templates involved, there are strong signals that the GhostPairing Attack is driven by a reusable kit that attackers can buy and distribute rather than a one-off project.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe same layout appears across multiple domains. The same idea of a Facebook viewer repeats, with minimal changes. Domain names follow a similar pattern around photos and posts, with a mix of generic TLDs.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eFrom an operational point of view, this is convenient. If one domain is blocked or reported, it can be discarded and replaced with another one using the same files. A buyer of such a kit can plug in their own configuration – for example text targeting a specific region or, as the scam propagates, text specific to an individual –\u0026nbsp;and start sending lures.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eWhat people can do to protect themselves\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eFor individuals, the most important actions are simple and do not require technical knowledge.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eFirst, it is worth checking which devices are currently linked:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003eOpen WhatsApp.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eGo to \u003cstrong\u003eSettings → Linked Devices\u003c/strong\u003e.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eReview the list of active sessions and log out of anything you do not recognise.\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cp\u003eDoing this once will remove any sessions already created by this sort of scam. Doing it periodically helps catch future problems earlier.\u003c/p\u003e"},{"bundle":"image","images":[{"sources":[{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_large/public/2025-12/6.jpg.webp?itok=3oqdxM2K 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_large_2x/public/2025-12/6.jpg.webp?itok=ZdaZZhqk 2x","media":"(min-width: 1020px)","type":"image/webp","width":308,"height":632},{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_medium/public/2025-12/6.jpg.webp?itok=qfOdWwwC 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_medium_2x/public/2025-12/6.jpg.webp?itok=Eh2UXPXh 2x","media":"(min-width: 720px)","type":"image/webp","width":308,"height":632},{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small/public/2025-12/6.jpg.webp?itok=b9OMAJ5w 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small_2x/public/2025-12/6.jpg.webp?itok=rwOhVwhN 2x","media":"(min-width: 420px)","type":"image/webp","width":308,"height":632}],"caption":"Linked devices showing an unknown browser session","url":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small/public/2025-12/6.jpg.webp?itok=b9OMAJ5w","link":""}]},{"bundle":"rich_text","content":"\u003cp\u003eSecond, treat any request to scan a WhatsApp QR code or enter a numeric code from a webpage as suspicious. WhatsApp linking should happen from inside the app and in response to a deliberate action, not because some external site tells you that it is required to view a file.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThird, enabling Two Step Verification in WhatsApp adds an additional barrier to other kinds of account abuse. It is not a complete solution, but it reduces the range of what attackers can do once they are in.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eFinally, sharing information about this kind of scam with family members and group chats is surprisingly effective. Many victims click because they have never heard of anything like this. Once people know that “I found your photo” messages can hide an account linking trick, they are less likely to follow the script.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eWhat platforms could improve\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eWhile user awareness is essential, there are also changes that platforms can consider.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eClearer messaging around device linking would help. If users saw large, unavoidable text explaining that they are about to give a new device full access to all messages and media, some would hesitate.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eBetter context on linking and pairing events would also be useful. Showing device type, browser, approximate location, and the fact that the request originated from a website, not from the app, could nudge users to cancel when something feels off.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eRate limiting device linking attempts, especially those that involve many different phone numbers from the same infrastructure, would raise the cost for attackers. Auto revoking recent linked sessions when spam or abuse is confirmed on an account would shorten the window of exploitation.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eNone of these measures alone would eliminate such attacks, but together they would make them less attractive.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eBeyond WhatsApp: pairing as a broader attack surface\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eGhostPairing is not just a quirky one off trick in a single app. It is a case study in what happens when a pairing flow is abused at scale. WhatsApp is a natural target, with billions of users and a growing role in everyday and business communication, but the pattern behind GhostPairing is more general.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eConceptually, the same risk exists anywhere you have:\u003c/p\u003e\u003col\u003e\u003cli\u003eA QR code, code entry screen, or approval prompt that lets a new device connect to an existing account.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eA trusted primary device that scans or approves that request.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eA resulting session that is long lived and largely invisible to the user.\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ol\u003e\u003cp\u003eIf those three conditions line up, an attacker who can control the QR code or pairing prompt, and convince the victim to interact with it, has a realistic path to creating their own ghost device.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eMany modern services follow this pattern in one form or another, from messaging apps with web or desktop clients, to collaboration tools, social platforms and account ecosystems that rely on “approve on your phone” prompts. Most of them add extra checks, such as device details, locations, or number matching, which makes abuse harder but does not change the basic dependency: a single distracted click or code entry on the primary device can grant an unknown session elsewhere.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eWe are not claiming that GhostPairing style campaigns are already running across all these ecosystems. What we can say is:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003eThe design pattern that made GhostPairing possible is not unique to WhatsApp.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eAny platform that combines very easy pairing with low visibility of linked devices gives attackers something to work with.\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cp\u003eIn that sense, GhostPairing should be read as a warning, not just a WhatsApp incident. The more our digital lives depend on quick QR scans and “approve on your phone” flows, the more important it becomes to design these steps so that a single moment of inattention does not quietly create a ghost device that lives in the background for months.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eConclusion\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe campaign described here illustrates a subtle shift in how some attackers operate. Instead of breaking cryptography or circumventing authentication, they use the product as designed and persuade users to cooperate at just the right moment.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eBy abusing WhatsApp’s device linking features and wrapping them in a familiar looking flow, they achieve a clean and persistent account compromise with minimal technical work.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe technique is not limited to one country or one platform. Any service that allows device pairing through QR codes or numeric codes is a potential candidate for similar abuse.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eGhostPairing Attacks are a good example of how social engineering and legitimate features combine into very effective compromises. The attacker never breaks encryption, they simply convince the user to invite them in as a linked device.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe good news is that the defense is well within reach. \u003ca\u003eA combination of user awareness, simple hygiene checks and modest platform changes can significantly reduce the impact of this type of scam.\u003c/a\u003e\u003c/p\u003e"}],"itemState":"published","timeToRead":"17","translations":[],"originalLanguagePost":{},"mediaType":{"video":false,"audio":false},"socialMediaMetaImage":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/social_media_meta/public/2025-12/Stocksy_txpe08a537dYqu300_OriginalDelivery_2842949%20%281%29.jpg?h=fa1c963e\u0026itok=YUxoElQf","language":"en","heroImage":{"default":{"sources":[{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_hero_extra_wide/public/2025-12/Stocksy_txpe08a537dYqu300_OriginalDelivery_2842949%20%281%29.jpg.webp?h=fa1c963e\u0026itok=9jsuoSM8 fake shops fake trust\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eModern fake shops are not just about flashy discounts. They are designed to look legitimate at a glance.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eMany use polished templates and SSL certificates, so the little padlock icon appears in the browser. That padlock only means the connection is encrypted, not that the site is honest, but scammers know people trust it.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eSome fake shops seed third party review platforms with positive reviews to boost their scores. In our analysis we saw cases where those reviews were later removed by the platform for breaking guidelines, a strong sign they were fake. In at least one case, the scam site ran on a subdomain of an older, existing site and showed Black Friday deals alongside those disputed reviews. Piggybacking on an established domain is another way to look more credible.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eOn social media, scammers run ads that look like any other promotion in your feed. Watch out for strange spellings, extra words, or domains that do not match the brand name you expect.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eBe skeptical of “too good to be true” discounts\u003c/strong\u003e. Genuine retailers do run big Black Friday sales, but if a high demand product is half the usual price, treat it as suspicious.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eVerify reviews\u003c/strong\u003e.\u003cstrong\u003e \u003c/strong\u003eDo not rely only on the testimonials shown on the shop itself. Search the store name together with words like \"reviews\", \"scam\" or \"complaints\". Be wary if you only find a handful of overly positive comments.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eCheck contact and return information. \u003c/strong\u003eLegitimate shops make it easy to see who they are and how to reach them, for example a physical address, company name, clear return policy, and real customer service options. Vague contact details or only a web form are warning signs.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eUse security tools and safer payment methods. \u003c/strong\u003eEnable browser protection and make sure your security solutions are on so known scam domains are blocked before you land on them. When you pay, prefer options that offer buyer protection, such as credit card or trusted payment service, and avoid bank transfers, gift cards or cryptocurrency for online purchases.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ol\u003e\u003cp\u003eScammers treat Black Friday, Cyber Monday and the entire holiday shopping season as a business opportunity. They use the same tools, platforms and marketing channels as legitimate stores, just with a very different goal.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe good news is that you do not need to stop shopping online to stay safe. A bit of skepticism, a habit of checking before you click, and the right security tools in the background will go a long way to keep your money and data out of their
