hacking groups typically operate in isolation, each advancing its own nation’s goals. That’s why any sign of collaboration between them is cause for concern. Yet new evidence uncovered by Gen researchers suggests that two of the world’s most aggressive advanced persistent threat (APT) actors, Russia-aligned Gamaredon and North Korea’s Lazarus, may be operating on shared infrastructure.\u0026nbsp;\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThis discovery hints at something much bigger than mere technical overlap. It points to a possible new stage in cyber conflict, where geopolitical alliances are mirrored in shared digital operations.\u0026nbsp;\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eFrom allies on the battlefield to partners online\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eRussia and North Korea have maintained a long-standing partnership rooted in shared political and military interests. Moscow backed Pyongyang during and after the Korean War, and in 2024 both nations renewed that alliance through a Comprehensive Strategic Partnership that includes mutual defense commitments.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eSince 2022, Pyongyang has stepped up its support for Moscow, formally recognizing Russian-claimed territories in Ukraine and reportedly supplying munitions and troops. In 2024, Reuters reported that North Korean soldiers had been deployed to fight alongside Russian forces in Ukraine, a striking example of the two countries’ deepening cooperation.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eNow, we may be witnessing a digital extension of that alliance. On July 28, 2025, Gen’s internal monitoring systems detected a suspicious event linking \u003cstrong\u003eGamaredon\u003c/strong\u003e and \u003cstrong\u003eLazarus\u003c/strong\u003e activity through a shared IP address. The implications are significant: two state-backed actors from different countries may be coordinating at an operational level.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThis development aligns with broader patterns highlighted in the \u003ca href=\"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/insights/reports/threat-report-q3-2025\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\"\u003eQ3/2025 Threat Report\u003c/a\u003e, where state sponsored operations showed increasing sophistication, coordination, and diversification of infrastructure. While those observations were confined within national ecosystems, the Gamaredon–Lazarus overlap suggests that similar dynamics may now be emerging across national boundaries.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eBackground\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h2\u003e\u003ch3\u003eGamaredon\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eGamaredon \u003c/strong\u003eis a Russian-aligned APT active since at least 2013, primarily focused on cyber espionage. In 2021, the Security Service of Ukraine issued a \u003ca href=\"https://ssu.gov.ua/en/novyny/sbu-vstanovyla-khakeriv-fsb-yaki-zdiisnyly-ponad-5-tys-kiberatak-na-derzhavni-orhany-ukrainy\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\"\u003epress release\u003c/a\u003e, attributing several members of the group as part of Russia's Federal Security Service (FSB) 18th Information Security Center. Since its official inception, the group is believed to have conducted more than 5000 cyber-attacks, most of which targeted Ukrainian government agencies. However, with the onset of war in Ukraine, ESET \u003ca href=\"https://www.welivesecurity.com/en/eset-research/cyberespionage-gamaredon-way-analysis-toolset-used-spy-ukraine-2022-2023/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\"\u003ereported\u003c/a\u003e that Gamaredon expanded its operations to include NATO member states, likely aiming to disrupt military aid to Ukraine, underscoring the group’s prioritization of hybrid warfare.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eLazarus\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eLazarus \u003c/strong\u003eis a state-sponsored threat actor active since 2009 and widely believed to operate under North Korea’s government. Initially focused on cyber espionage and destructive attacks, Lazarus later shifted toward financially motivated operations to fund future campaigns. In 2021, the United States Department of Justice \u003ca href=\"https://www.justice.gov/archives/opa/pr/north-korean-regime-backed-programmer-charged-conspiracy-conduct-multiple-cyber-attacks-and\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\"\u003eindicted\u003c/a\u003e three members believed to be part of the Lazarus group, connecting them to North Korea’s Reconnaissance General Bureau (RGB), the country’s primary intelligence agency. With the rise of cryptocurrency, Lazarus increasingly targeted digital assets, as evidenced by high-profile breaches such as \u003ca href=\"https://www.fbi.gov/news/press-releases/fbi-identifies-lazarus-group-cyber-actors-as-responsible-for-theft-of-41-million-from-stakecom\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\"\u003eStake.com\u003c/a\u003e ($41 million), \u003ca href=\"https://www.fbi.gov/news/press-releases/fbi-identifies-cryptocurrency-funds-stolen-by-dprk\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\"\u003eAtomicWallet\u003c/a\u003e ($100 million), \u003ca href=\"https://www.businesstoday.in/technology/news/story/wazirx-hacked-north-korean-hackers-behind-235-million-theft-from-indian-investors-report-reveals-439240-2024-07-29\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\"\u003eWazirX\u003c/a\u003e ($235 million), and \u003ca href=\"https://neobanque.ch/blog/bybit-1-4-billion-hack-lazarus-group-2025/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\"\u003eBybit\u003c/a\u003e ($1.4 billion).\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eWhere \u003cstrong\u003eGamaredon spies, Lazarus steals\u003c/strong\u003e, but both ultimately serve their governments’ strategic interests.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eThe discovery: a shared digital footprint\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eJust one day after the \u003ca href=\"https://www.reuters.com/business/aerospace-defense/russia-starts-first-moscow-pyongyang-passenger-flights-decades-2025-07-27/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\"\u003eannouncement\u003c/a\u003e of new direct flights between Moscow and Pyongyang, Gen identified indicators of a potential collaboration between the Gamaredon and Lazarus APTs. On July 24, 2025, our system tracking Gamaredon’s Command-and-Control (C2) servers via known Telegram and Telegraph channels blocked an IP address:\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e144[.]172[.]112[.]106\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e"},{"bundle":"image","images":[{"sources":[{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_large/public/2025-11/1.png.webp?itok=9QhO-lvd 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_large_2x/public/2025-11/1.png.webp?itok=9cTN8qMb 2x","media":"(min-width: 1020px)","type":"image/webp","width":647,"height":27},{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_medium/public/2025-11/1.png.webp?itok=Xhx1_gKk 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_medium_2x/public/2025-11/1.png.webp?itok=PS7yGsb1 2x","media":"(min-width: 720px)","type":"image/webp","width":647,"height":27},{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small/public/2025-11/1.png.webp?itok=TFWMla6Q 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small_2x/public/2025-11/1.png.webp?itok=rs0aLUYf 2x","media":"(min-width: 420px)","type":"image/webp","width":647,"height":27}],"caption":"","url":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small/public/2025-11/1.png.webp?itok=TFWMla6Q","link":""}]},{"bundle":"rich_text","content":"\u003cp\u003eFour days later, during a routine check, the same server was found hosting an obfuscated version of \u003cstrong\u003eInvisibleFerret \u003c/strong\u003e(SHA256: `128da948f7c3a6c052e782acfee503383bf05d953f3db5c603e4d386e2cf4b4d`), a malware strain attributed to \u003cstrong\u003eLazarus\u003c/strong\u003e. The payload matched Lazarus’ tooling and was delivered through an identical server structure (URL: `http[://]144[.]172[.]112[.]106/payload/99/81`) previously seen in \u003cem\u003eContagiousInterview\u003c/em\u003e, a Lazarus campaign that targeted job seekers with fake recruitment messages. While the IP could represent a proxy or VPN endpoint, the temporal proximity of both groups’ activity and the shared hosting pattern indicate probable infrastructure reuse, with moderate confidence of operational collaboration. Whether Lazarus leveraged a Gamaredon-controlled server or both actors shared the same client instance remains unclear, but the overlap is too close to ignore.\u0026nbsp;\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eImplications for the global threat landscape\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eCross-country collaborations in the APT ecosystem remain exceptionally rare. The last widely acknowledged example dates back to 2014 with the \u003ca href=\"https://theintercept.com/2014/11/24/secret-regin-malware-belgacom-nsa-gchq/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\"\u003eRegin\u003c/a\u003e malware, reportedly co-developed by the U.S. National Security Agency (NSA) and the U.K.’s Government Communications Headquarters (GCHQ).\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eIf confirmed, the Gamaredon–Lazarus overlap would represent the first known case of \u003cstrong\u003eRussian–North Korean cyber collaboration\u003c/strong\u003e in the wild.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eSuch a partnership could have wide-ranging implications:\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eOperational synergy:\u003c/strong\u003e Lazarus’s expertise in monetizing cyberattacks through cryptocurrency theft could help Gamaredon fund or conceal future operations.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eStrategic alignment:\u003c/strong\u003e Russia, facing mounting economic and military pressure, could benefit from North Korea’s established infrastructure for covert financial operations.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eEscalation potential:\u003c/strong\u003e This kind of collaboration blurs the line between espionage, sabotage, and organized cybercrime, expanding both nations’ offensive reach.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003ch2\u003eNot an isolated case: national ecosystems are merging\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eWhile cross-border APT collaboration is rare, cooperation within national ecosystems has become increasingly common.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eLazarus x Kimsuky\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eKimsuky \u003c/strong\u003eis another North Korean APT group. It has been active since around 2012 and assessed by \u003ca href=\"https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/mapping-dprk-groups-to-government\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\"\u003eMandiant\u003c/a\u003e to operate under the RGB. The group specializes in advanced cyber-espionage campaigns, primarily targeting government entities and consumer-facing organizations.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eDuring analysis of Lazarus’ \u003cem\u003eContagiousInterview\u003c/em\u003e payloads, Gen researchers found that an IP address (216[.]219[.]87[.]41) later reappeared in Kimsuky-linked payloads (e.g., cce27340fd6f32d96c65b7b1034c65d5026d7d0b96c80bcf31e40ab4b8834bcd). This suggests infrastructure reuse or coordination between RGB units, evidence of alignment at North Korea’s national level.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eDoNot x SideWinder\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003ca href=\"https://www.amnesty.org/en/latest/news/2021/10/togo-activist-targeted-with-spyware-by-notorious-hacker-group/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eDoNot\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e \u003c/strong\u003eand \u003ca href=\"https://cdn-cybersecurity.att.com/docs/global-perspective-of-the-sidewinder-apt.pdf\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eSideWinder\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e \u003c/strong\u003eare state-sponsored APT groups believed to have been active since 2013 and 2012, respectively, both with ties to the Indian government and a primary focus on cyber espionage.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eGen identified a DoNot-attributed payload (8bb089d763d5d4b4f96ae59eb9d8f919e6a49611c183f636bfd5c01696447938) that later executed a known SideWinder loader (f4d10604980f8f556440460adc71883f04e24231d0a9a3a323a86651405bedfb). The victim was located in Pakistan, consistent with the typical targeting profile of both groups. Historically, cross-country APT collaborations have been exceedingly rare, with only a handful of confirmed cases such as Stuxnet and Regin. This potential partnership signals a shift toward more complex and unpredictable alliances, where geopolitical interests may drive operational convergence.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eWhile the Lazarus–Gamaredon case stands out for its strategic implications, the observed intranational collaborations, such as Lazarus with Kimsuky and DoNot with SideWinder, are equally important. These partnerships demonstrate a growing trend of resource sharing and tactical alignment within national ecosystems, amplifying the reach and resilience of state-sponsored campaigns.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eFor defenders, these findings underscore an urgent need to adapt detection strategies beyond single-actor attribution. Shared infrastructure, overlapping TTPs, and modular malware frameworks mean that traditional attribution models may fail to capture the full scope of risk. Security teams must:\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003eEnhance infrastructure correlation analysis to detect cross-group overlaps early. \u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003ePrioritize intelligence sharing across organizations and sectors to identify emerging alliances. \u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eImplement layered defenses capable of mitigating diverse tactics from multiple threat actors leveraging common resources.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe era of isolated APT operations is fading. As adversaries evolve through collaboration, defenders must respond with equal agility and cooperation to safeguard critical recent weeks, our Threat Labs researchers have uncovered two new toolsets that show just how adaptive the DPRK’s operations have become. Kimsuky, known for its espionage-style campaigns, deployed a new backdoor we’ve named \u003cstrong\u003eHttpTroy\u003c/strong\u003e, while Lazarus introduced an upgraded version of its \u003cstrong\u003eBLINDINGCAN\u003c/strong\u003e remote access tool.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eBoth attacks reveal the same underlying pattern: stealthy code and layered obfuscation. In this post, we’ll break down how these tools work, what they target and what defenders can learn from the latest moves inside the DPRK playbook.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eInside DPRK’s Latest Campaigns: How Kimsuky and Lazarus Refine Their Playbook\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe Kimsuky attack targeted a single victim in KR and started with a ZIP file that looked like a VPN invoice, then quietly installed tools that let attackers move files, take screenshots and run commands. The chain has three steps: a small dropper, a loader called MemLoad, and the final backdoor, named “HttpTroy”. We see several signs that possibly tie this activity to Kimsuky: the Korean language lure, an AhnLab-style scheduled task name and command patterns seen in past Kimsuky work.\u0026nbsp;\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe Lazarus attack targeted two victims in CA and was caught in the middle of the attack chain, where we observed a new version of the Comebacker malware leading to a new variant of their BLINDINGCAN remote access tool.\u0026nbsp;\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eWe’ll explain how these attacks work in plain terms, why North Korea-aligned groups use these tricks, and simple steps you can take to avoid similar threats.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eQuick takeaways for readers:\u003c/strong\u003e do not open any attachments you did not expect, treat .scr files as programs not documents and keep your security software on and updated.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eDiving into the Kimsuky attack: MemLoad and HttpTroy\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h2\u003e\u003ch3\u003eThe Initial dropper\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eWhile the exact delivery mechanism remains unknown, telemetry indicates that the samples was obtained via an internet download, packaged within a ZIP archive named “\u003cstrong\u003e250908_A_HK이노션_SecuwaySSL VPN Manager U100S 100user_견적서\u003c/strong\u003e”. Given the nature of the filename, it is highly probable that the archive was distributed through a phishing email.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eContained within the archive is a “.scr” file bearing the same name. Execution of this file initiates the entire infection chain.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThis initial sample is a lightweight GO binary containing 3 embedded files. These files are decrypted using a simple XOR operation with the key “0x39”, then written to disk and executed. To maintain user deception, the binary displays a PDF document as a decoy, displaying a fake bill for VPN services, while simultaneously registering the next stage backdoor as a COM server using “regsvr32.exe”. and Lazarus continue to sharpen their tools, showing that DPRK-linked actors aren’t just maintaining their arsenals, they’re reinventing them. These campaigns demonstrate a well-structured and multi-stage infection chain, leveraging obfuscated payloads and stealthy persistence mechanisms. From the initial stages to the final backdoors, each component is designed to evade detection, maintain access and provide extensive control over the compromised system. The use of custom encryption, dynamic API resolution and COM-based task registration/services exploitation highlights the groups’ continued evolution and technical sophistication.
